Similarly, the acceleration of a point in the rotating frame can be described using the angular acceleration matrix.



### Subsection: 11.2c Challenges in Fixed-axis Rotation



While fixed-axis rotation is a fundamental concept in dynamics and control, it also presents some challenges in modeling and analysis. One of the main challenges is dealing with non-inertial reference frames. In fixed-axis rotation, the reference frame is rotating, which means that it is not an inertial frame of reference. This can complicate the equations of motion and make it more difficult to analyze the system.



Another challenge is dealing with the effects of external forces on the rotating object. In a non-rotating object, external forces can be easily accounted for by using Newton's laws of motion. However, in a rotating object, these forces can cause additional torques and moments that must be considered in the equations of motion.



In addition, fixed-axis rotation can also lead to gyroscopic effects, which can have a significant impact on the behavior of the system. Gyroscopic effects occur when a rotating object experiences a force that is perpendicular to both the axis of rotation and the direction of the force. This can cause the object to precess, or rotate around a different axis, which can complicate the analysis of the system.



Despite these challenges, fixed-axis rotation remains an important concept in dynamics and control, and understanding its complexities is crucial for engineers and scientists working with rotating systems. In the next section, we will explore some applications of fixed-axis rotation and see how it is used in real-world engineering systems.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 11: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 11.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will continue our review of 2.003 by discussing first and second order systems. These types of systems are commonly encountered in engineering and are essential for understanding the dynamics and control of many physical systems.



#### 11.3a Understanding First Order Systems



First order systems are characterized by a single differential equation that relates the input and output of the system. They are often used to model systems with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. In these systems, the output is directly proportional to the input, with a time constant that depends on the energy storage element.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the example of an RC circuit. In this circuit, a resistor (R) and a capacitor (C) are connected in series with a voltage source. The voltage across the capacitor (Vc) can be described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_{in} - V_c)

$$



where V_{in} is the input voltage and RC is the time constant of the circuit. This equation shows that the rate of change of the output voltage is directly proportional to the difference between the input voltage and the current voltage across the capacitor.



Another important concept in first order systems is the step response. This is the response of the system to a sudden change in the input, such as a step function. In the case of the RC circuit, the step response can be calculated using the following equation:



$$

V_c(t) = V_{in}(1-e^{-\frac{t}{RC}})

$$



This equation shows that the output voltage will approach the input voltage exponentially with a time constant of RC. This is a fundamental property of first order systems and is used to analyze and design control systems.



Moving on to second order systems, these are characterized by two differential equations that relate the input and output of the system. They are often used to model systems with two energy storage elements, such as a mass-spring-damper system. In these systems, the output is influenced by both the input and the state of the system, making them more complex than first order systems.



To better understand second order systems, let's consider the example of a mass-spring-damper system. In this system, a mass (m) is connected to a spring (k) and a damper (b). The displacement of the mass (x) can be described by the following second order differential equation:



$$

m\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} + b\frac{dx}{dt} + kx = F_{in}

$$



where F_{in} is the input force. This equation shows that the acceleration of the mass is influenced by both the input force and the current displacement and velocity of the mass.



Similar to first order systems, the step response of a second order system can also be calculated. However, in this case, the response will depend on the damping ratio of the system, which is a measure of how much the system is damped. A higher damping ratio results in a slower response and less oscillation, while a lower damping ratio results in a faster response and more oscillation.



In conclusion, first and second order systems are fundamental concepts in the study of dynamics and control. They are used to model a wide range of physical systems and are essential for understanding the behavior and designing control strategies for these systems. In the next section, we will discuss more advanced systems and their applications in engineering.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 11: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 11.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will continue our review of 2.003 by discussing first and second order systems. These types of systems are commonly encountered in engineering and are essential for understanding the dynamics and control of many physical systems.



#### 11.3a Understanding First Order Systems



First order systems are characterized by a single differential equation that relates the input and output of the system. They are often used to model systems with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. In these systems, the output is directly proportional to the input, with a time constant that depends on the energy storage element.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the example of an RC circuit. In this circuit, a resistor (R) and a capacitor (C) are connected in series with a voltage source. The voltage across the capacitor (Vc) can be described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_{in} - V_c)

$$



where V_{in} is the input voltage and RC is the time constant of the circuit. This equation shows that the rate of change of the output voltage is directly proportional to the difference between the input voltage and the current voltage across the capacitor.



Another important concept in first order systems is the step response. This is the response of the system to a sudden change in the input, such as a step function. In the case of the RC circuit, the step response can be calculated using the following equation:



$$

V_c(t) = V_{in}(1-e^{-\frac{t}{RC}})

$$



This equation shows that the output voltage will approach the input voltage exponentially with a time constant of RC. This is a fundamental property of first order systems and is used to analyze and design control systems.



Moving on to second order systems, these are characterized by two differential equations that relate the input and output of the system. They are commonly used to model systems with two energy storage elements, such as a mass-spring-damper system. The equations of motion for a second order system can be written as:



$$

\ddot{x} + 2\zeta\omega_n\dot{x} + \omega_n^2x = u

$$



where x is the output, u is the input, and \zeta and \omega_n are the damping ratio and natural frequency of the system, respectively. The damping ratio \zeta determines the type of response of the system, whether it is underdamped, critically damped, or overdamped.



Similar to first order systems, the step response of a second order system can also be calculated. In the case of a mass-spring-damper system, the step response can be written as:



$$

x(t) = \frac{u}{\omega_n^2}(1-e^{-\zeta\omega_nt}) + \frac{\dot{u} + \zeta\omega_nu}{\omega_n^2}e^{-\zeta\omega_nt}

$$



This equation shows that the output will approach the input with a time constant of \zeta\omega_n. The behavior of second order systems is more complex than first order systems, but they are still fundamental in understanding the dynamics and control of many physical systems.



In conclusion, first and second order systems are essential for understanding the dynamics and control of physical systems. They are commonly encountered in engineering and have important applications in various fields. By understanding the properties and behaviors of these systems, we can better analyze and design control systems for a wide range of applications.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 11: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 11.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will continue our review of 2.003 by discussing first and second order systems. These types of systems are commonly encountered in engineering and are essential for understanding the dynamics and control of many physical systems.



#### 11.3a Understanding First Order Systems



First order systems are characterized by a single differential equation that relates the input and output of the system. They are often used to model systems with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. In these systems, the output is directly proportional to the input, with a time constant that depends on the energy storage element.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the example of an RC circuit. In this circuit, a resistor (R) and a capacitor (C) are connected in series with a voltage source. The voltage across the capacitor (Vc) can be described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_{in} - V_c)

$$



where V_{in} is the input voltage and RC is the time constant of the circuit. This equation shows that the rate of change of the output voltage is directly proportional to the difference between the input voltage and the current voltage across the capacitor.



Another important concept in first order systems is the step response. This is the response of the system to a sudden change in the input, such as a step function. In the case of the RC circuit, the step response can be calculated using the following equation:



$$

V_c(t) = V_{in}(1-e^{-\frac{t}{RC}})

$$



This equation shows that the output voltage will approach the input voltage exponentially with a time constant of RC. This is a fundamental property of first order systems and is used to analyze and design control systems.



#### 11.3b Understanding Second Order Systems



Second order systems are characterized by two differential equations that relate the input and output of the system. They are often used to model systems with two energy storage elements, such as a mass-spring-damper system. In these systems, the output is influenced by both the input and the state of the system, making them more complex than first order systems.



To better understand second order systems, let's consider the example of a mass-spring-damper system. In this system, a mass (m) is attached to a spring (k) and a damper (b). The position of the mass (x) can be described by the following second order differential equation:



$$

m\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} + b\frac{dx}{dt} + kx = F_{in}

$$



where F_{in} is the input force. This equation shows that the acceleration of the mass is influenced by both the input force and the current position and velocity of the mass.



Similar to first order systems, second order systems also have a step response. However, the step response for a second order system is more complex and can be calculated using the following equation:



$$

x(t) = \frac{F_{in}}{k}(1-e^{-\frac{bt}{2m}}\cos(\omega t + \phi))

$$



where \omega = \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}} is the natural frequency of the system and \phi is the phase angle. This equation shows that the output of a second order system will oscillate around the input value before settling to the steady-state value.



### 11.3c Comparing First and Second Order Systems



Now that we have a better understanding of first and second order systems, let's compare them. First order systems are simpler and easier to analyze, but they are limited in their ability to model complex systems. Second order systems, on the other hand, are more complex but can model a wider range of systems.



One key difference between first and second order systems is their step response. First order systems have a single time constant that determines how quickly the output will approach the input value. Second order systems, on the other hand, have two time constants - the natural frequency and the damping ratio - that determine the behavior of the step response.



Another difference is the presence of oscillations in the output of second order systems. This is due to the presence of the natural frequency, which causes the system to oscillate around the input value before settling to the steady-state value. First order systems do not exhibit this behavior.



In conclusion, first and second order systems have their own unique characteristics and are suitable for modeling different types of systems. It is important for engineers to understand the differences between these two types of systems in order to effectively analyze and design control systems.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction



In the previous chapter, we discussed the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. In this chapter, we will extend our understanding to more complex systems by exploring the plane-motion dynamics of rigid bodies. This topic is crucial in the field of engineering as it allows us to analyze and design control systems for a wide range of mechanical systems, such as airplanes, satellites, and robots.



The motion of rigid bodies in a plane is governed by the principles of Newtonian mechanics, which state that the net force and torque acting on a body determine its translational and rotational motion, respectively. We will begin by reviewing the basic concepts of rigid body dynamics, including the definition of a rigid body, its degrees of freedom, and the equations of motion. We will then move on to discuss the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit, such as translation, rotation, and general plane motion.



Next, we will delve into the topic of kinematics, which deals with the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. We will learn about the different methods of describing the motion of a rigid body, including the use of position, velocity, and acceleration vectors, as well as the use of angular displacement, velocity, and acceleration. We will also explore the concept of relative motion and how it can be used to analyze complex systems.



Finally, we will apply our knowledge of rigid body dynamics and kinematics to the design and analysis of control systems. We will discuss the different types of control systems, such as open-loop and closed-loop systems, and how they can be used to stabilize and control the motion of a rigid body. We will also cover the concept of stability and how it relates to the design of control systems.



By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the dynamics and control of rigid bodies in a plane, and you will be able to apply this knowledge to a wide range of engineering problems. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of plane-motion dynamics of rigid bodies.

This includes translation, which is the motion of a body along a straight line, and rotation, which is the motion of a body about a fixed axis. ## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in 2.003, an introductory course on dynamics and control at MIT. This will serve as a refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



#### 12.1a Overview of 2.003



2.003 is a fundamental course that introduces students to the principles of dynamics and control. It is a continuation of the first course, 2.001, which covers statics and basic dynamics. In 2.003, we build upon the concepts learned in 2.001 and apply them to more complex systems. The course is designed to provide students with a strong foundation in these concepts, which are essential for understanding the dynamics and control of mechanical systems.



The course begins with an introduction to the concept of a rigid body, which is a body that maintains its shape and size even when subjected to external forces. We will review the definition of a rigid body and its degrees of freedom, which are the minimum number of independent coordinates required to describe its motion. This will be followed by a discussion on the equations of motion for a rigid body, which are derived from Newton's laws of motion.



Next, we will explore the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit. This includes translation, which is the motion of a body along a straight line, and rotation, which is the motion of a body about a fixed axis. We will also discuss general plane motion, which is a combination of translation and rotation.



After reviewing the basics of rigid body dynamics, we will move on to kinematics, which is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. We will learn about the different methods of describing the motion of a rigid body, including the use of position, velocity, and acceleration vectors, as well as the use of angular displacement, velocity, and acceleration. These concepts will be essential for understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies in later sections.



#### 12.1b Equations of Motion for a Rigid Body



In 2.003, we learned that the equations of motion for a rigid body can be derived from Newton's laws of motion. These laws state that the sum of all forces acting on a body is equal to its mass times its acceleration. For a rigid body, this can be expressed as:



$$

\Sigma F = m\ddot{x}

$$



Where $\Sigma F$ is the sum of all external forces acting on the body, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\ddot{x}$ is the acceleration of the body's center of mass.



We also learned that the equations of motion can be expressed in terms of angular quantities. For a rigid body rotating about a fixed axis, the equations of motion can be written as:



$$

\Sigma M = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



Where $\Sigma M$ is the sum of all external torques acting on the body, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body about the axis of rotation, and $\ddot{\theta}$ is the angular acceleration of the body.



#### 12.1c Applications from 2.003



In 2.003, we studied the dynamics of various mechanical systems, including pendulums, springs, and gears. We learned how to model these systems using the equations of motion and how to analyze their behavior using principles of dynamics and control.



One of the key applications of the concepts learned in 2.003 is in the design and control of robots. By understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies, we can design robots that can move and manipulate objects in a controlled manner. We can also use control theory to develop algorithms that allow robots to perform complex tasks.



Another important application is in the design of vehicles, such as airplanes and cars. By understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies, we can design vehicles that are stable and maneuverable. We can also use control theory to develop systems that improve the performance and safety of these vehicles.



In later sections, we will explore these and other applications in more detail, building upon the foundation laid in 2.003. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will delve deeper into the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. As a refresher, kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. In the previous section, we briefly discussed the different methods of describing the motion of a rigid body. Now, we will focus on understanding the kinematics of plane motion in more detail.



#### 12.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



To begin, let us define plane motion as the motion of a rigid body in a plane, where all points in the body move in parallel planes. This type of motion is commonly seen in systems such as aircraft, cars, and robots. In order to fully understand plane-motion kinematics, we must first define the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit.



The first type of motion is translation, which is the motion of a body along a straight line. This type of motion is characterized by a constant velocity and zero angular velocity. In other words, all points in the body move in the same direction and at the same speed. This type of motion is commonly seen in systems such as cars and trains.



The second type of motion is rotation, which is the motion of a body about a fixed axis. This type of motion is characterized by a constant angular velocity and zero linear velocity. In other words, all points in the body move in circular paths around the fixed axis at the same speed. This type of motion is commonly seen in systems such as propellers and wheels.



Finally, we have general plane motion, which is a combination of translation and rotation. This type of motion is characterized by both linear and angular velocities. In this case, different points in the body may have different velocities and directions of motion. This type of motion is commonly seen in systems such as helicopters and robots.



Now that we have defined the different types of motion, let us discuss the methods of describing the motion of a rigid body. The most common method is using position, velocity, and acceleration vectors. These vectors describe the location, speed, and change in speed of a point on the body, respectively. Another method is using angular displacement, velocity, and acceleration, which describe the rotation of the body about a fixed axis.



In conclusion, understanding the kinematics of plane motion is crucial for analyzing and designing systems that exhibit this type of motion. By defining the different types of motion and methods of describing them, we can better understand the behavior of rigid bodies in plane motion. In the next section, we will apply these concepts to the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion.

## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:

One of the main applications of plane-motion kinematics is in the design and analysis of factory automation infrastructure. This includes systems such as robotic arms, conveyor belts, and assembly lines. By understanding the kinematics of plane motion, engineers can optimize the movement and coordination of these systems to increase efficiency and productivity.



Another important application is in the visualization of the rotation of spacecraft in orbit. Poinsot's ellipsoid, also known as the kinetic width, is a geometric construction that is used to visualize the rotation of a spacecraft. This is based on the principles of plane-motion kinematics and is a valuable tool for engineers and scientists in the aerospace industry.



In addition, plane-motion kinematics is also used in the design and analysis of various vehicles, such as cars, aircraft, and trains. By understanding the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit, engineers can design and optimize the movement of these vehicles for safe and efficient operation.



Further reading on this topic can be found in the paper "Maintaining the extent of a moving set of points" by P. K. Agarwal, L. J. Guibas, J. Hershberger, and E. Verach. This paper discusses the use of plane-motion kinematics in maintaining the extent of a moving set of points, which has applications in various fields such as computer graphics and robotics.

# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will continue our discussion on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. As we have previously defined, plane motion is the motion of a rigid body in a plane, where all points in the body move in parallel planes. In this section, we will explore the applications of plane-motion kinematics and how it can be used to analyze and model various systems.



#### 12.2b Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



One of the main applications of plane-motion kinematics is in the design and analysis of factory automation infrastructure. This includes systems such as robotic arms, conveyor belts, and assembly lines. By understanding the kinematics of plane motion, engineers can optimize the movement and coordination of these systems to increase efficiency and productivity.



Another important application is in the visualization of the rotation of spacecraft in orbit. Poinsot's ellipsoid, also known as the kinetic width, is a geometric construction that is used to visualize the rotation of a spacecraft. This is based on the principles of plane-motion kinematics and is a valuable tool for engineers and scientists in the aerospace industry.



In addition, plane-motion kinematics is also used in the design and analysis of various vehicles, such as cars, aircraft, and trains. By understanding the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit, engineers can design and optimize the movement of these vehicles for safe and efficient operation.



Further reading on this topic can be found in the paper "Maintaining the extent of a moving set of points" by P. K. Agarwal, L. J. Guibas, J. Hershberger, and E. Verach. This paper discusses the use of plane-motion kinematics in maintaining the extent of a moving set of points, which has applications in various fields such as computer graphics and robotics.



#### 12.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



While plane-motion kinematics has many practical applications, there are also challenges that arise when trying to model and analyze systems using this concept. One of the main challenges is dealing with non-ideal conditions, such as friction and external forces, which can affect the motion of a rigid body. These factors can make it difficult to accurately predict the behavior of a system and may require additional considerations in the modeling process.



Another challenge is the complexity of the equations involved in plane-motion kinematics. As the number of degrees of freedom increases, the equations become more complex and may require advanced mathematical techniques to solve. This can make it challenging for engineers to accurately model and analyze systems with a large number of moving parts.



Furthermore, the assumptions made in plane-motion kinematics, such as rigid bodies and ideal conditions, may not always hold true in real-world scenarios. This can lead to discrepancies between the predicted and actual behavior of a system, making it important for engineers to carefully consider the limitations of plane-motion kinematics when applying it to real-world problems.



Despite these challenges, plane-motion kinematics remains a valuable tool in the field of dynamics and control, providing a framework for understanding and analyzing the motion of rigid bodies in a plane. By carefully considering the limitations and challenges, engineers can effectively use this concept to design and optimize various systems for a wide range of applications.





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of plane motion for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and how they can be modeled and controlled.



#### 12.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of plane motion, we must first define the concept of a rigid body. A rigid body is a body that maintains its shape and size, and all points in the body move in parallel planes. This means that the distance between any two points in the body remains constant throughout its motion.



The motion of a rigid body can be described by its translational and rotational motion. Translational motion refers to the movement of the body as a whole, while rotational motion refers to the rotation of the body about a fixed axis. In plane motion, both translational and rotational motion occur in a single plane.



To model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we can use the principles of Newtonian mechanics. Newton's laws of motion state that an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion unless acted upon by an external force. This means that to change the motion of a rigid body, we must apply a force to it.



In addition to external forces, we must also consider internal forces within the rigid body. These internal forces, such as tension and compression, can affect the motion of the body and must be taken into account in our modeling.



One way to model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion is through the use of free body diagrams. These diagrams show all the external and internal forces acting on the body and can help us analyze the motion and determine the equations of motion.



Another important aspect of modeling dynamics is understanding the concept of moments of inertia. Moments of inertia are a measure of an object's resistance to rotational motion and are crucial in determining the rotational dynamics of a rigid body.



In the next section, we will explore the applications of plane-motion dynamics and how it can be used to analyze and control various systems. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of plane motion for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and how they can be modeled and controlled.



#### 12.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of plane motion, we must first define the concept of a rigid body. A rigid body is a body that maintains its shape and size, and all points in the body move in parallel planes. This means that the distance between any two points in the body remains constant throughout its motion.



The motion of a rigid body can be described by its translational and rotational motion. Translational motion refers to the movement of the body as a whole, while rotational motion refers to the rotation of the body about a fixed axis. In plane motion, both translational and rotational motion occur in a single plane.



To model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we can use the principles of Newtonian mechanics. Newton's laws of motion state that an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion unless acted upon by an external force. This means that to change the motion of a rigid body, we must apply a force to it.



In addition to external forces, we must also consider internal forces within the rigid body. These internal forces, such as tension and compression, can affect the motion of the body and must be taken into account in our modeling.



One way to model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion is through the use of free body diagrams. These diagrams show all the external and internal forces acting on the body and can help us analyze the motion and determine the equations of motion.



Another important aspect of modeling dynamics is understanding the concept of moments of inertia. Moments of inertia are a measure of an object's resistance to rotational motion and are crucial in determining the rotational dynamics of a rigid body. They are defined as the sum of the products of the mass of each particle in the body and the square of its distance from the axis of rotation. The moment of inertia is dependent on the shape and mass distribution of the body, and it can be calculated using integrals or tables for common shapes.



To fully model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we must also consider the conservation of energy and momentum. These principles can be applied to the translational and rotational motion of the body, and they provide additional equations to solve for the motion of the body.



In summary, understanding the dynamics of plane motion for rigid bodies involves considering external and internal forces, using free body diagrams, and applying principles of Newtonian mechanics, moments of inertia, and conservation laws. By combining these concepts, we can accurately model and control the motion of rigid bodies in plane motion.





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 12: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 12.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of plane motion for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and how they can be modeled and controlled.



#### 12.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of plane motion, we must first define the concept of a rigid body. A rigid body is a body that maintains its shape and size, and all points in the body move in parallel planes. This means that the distance between any two points in the body remains constant throughout its motion.



The motion of a rigid body can be described by its translational and rotational motion. Translational motion refers to the movement of the body as a whole, while rotational motion refers to the rotation of the body about a fixed axis. In plane motion, both translational and rotational motion occur in a single plane.



To model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we can use the principles of Newtonian mechanics. Newton's laws of motion state that an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion unless acted upon by an external force. This means that to change the motion of a rigid body, we must apply a force to it.



In addition to external forces, we must also consider internal forces within the rigid body. These internal forces, such as tension and compression, can affect the motion of the body and must be taken into account in our modeling.



One way to model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion is through the use of free body diagrams. These diagrams show all the external and internal forces acting on the body and can help us analyze the motion and determine the equations of motion.



Another important aspect of modeling dynamics is understanding the concept of moments of inertia. Moments of inertia are a measure of an object's resistance to rotational motion and are dependent on the mass distribution and shape of the object. In plane motion, we must consider both the moment of inertia about the axis of rotation and the moment of inertia about the perpendicular axis.



To fully understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we must also consider the concept of torque. Torque is the rotational equivalent of force and is defined as the product of force and the distance from the axis of rotation. In plane motion, torque can be caused by both external forces and internal forces, such as friction.



#### 12.3b Modeling Plane-motion Dynamics



To model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we can use the principles of Newtonian mechanics and the equations of motion. These equations describe the relationship between the forces acting on the body and its resulting motion.



For translational motion, we can use Newton's second law, which states that the sum of all external forces acting on an object is equal to its mass times its acceleration. In plane motion, we must consider both the x and y components of this equation.



For rotational motion, we can use the principle of conservation of angular momentum, which states that the total angular momentum of a system remains constant unless acted upon by an external torque. In plane motion, we must consider both the x and y components of this equation, as well as the moment of inertia and the angular acceleration.



To fully model the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we must also consider the concept of energy. In plane motion, we can use the principle of conservation of energy, which states that the total energy of a system remains constant. This can be useful in analyzing the motion of a rigid body and determining its final position and velocity.



#### 12.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



While the principles and equations of motion provide a solid foundation for modeling the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, there are still challenges that must be addressed. One of the main challenges is accurately modeling the internal forces within the body, such as friction and deformation.



Another challenge is dealing with non-rigid bodies, which do not maintain their shape and size during motion. These bodies require more complex modeling techniques, such as finite element analysis, to accurately predict their motion.



In addition, the assumptions made in modeling, such as the rigid body assumption, may not always hold true in real-world scenarios. This can lead to errors in the predicted motion of the body.



Despite these challenges, the principles and equations of motion provide a strong foundation for understanding and modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. With further advancements in technology and modeling techniques, we can continue to improve our understanding and control of these systems.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. We began by discussing the concept of inertia and how it relates to the motion of a rigid body. We then introduced the equations of motion for a rigid body, including the moment of inertia and the angular momentum. We also discussed the principle of conservation of angular momentum and how it can be applied to solve problems involving rigid bodies in motion.



Next, we delved into the dynamics of a rigid body in free fall, examining the forces and torques acting on the body and how they affect its motion. We also explored the concept of rotational kinetic energy and how it relates to the motion of a rigid body. We then moved on to discuss the dynamics of a rigid body in circular motion, including the centripetal force and the concept of angular velocity.



Finally, we applied our knowledge of rigid body dynamics to solve real-world problems, such as the motion of a spinning top and the motion of a gyroscope. We also discussed the stability of a spinning body and how it can be affected by external forces.



Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. By mastering these concepts, readers will be able to apply them to a wide range of engineering and physics problems.

#### Exercise 1

A uniform rod of length $L$ and mass $m$ is pivoted at one end and released from rest in a vertical position. Find the angular velocity of the rod when it makes an angle $\theta$ with the vertical.



#### Exercise 2

A disc of radius $R$ and mass $m$ is rolling without slipping on a horizontal surface. Find the acceleration of the center of mass and the angular acceleration of the disc.



#### Exercise 3

A solid sphere of radius $R$ and mass $m$ is rolling without slipping on a horizontal surface. Find the kinetic energy of the sphere.



#### Exercise 4

A thin rod of length $L$ and mass $m$ is pivoted at one end and released from rest in a horizontal position. Find the angular velocity of the rod when it makes an angle $\theta$ with the horizontal.



#### Exercise 5

A uniform cylinder of radius $R$ and mass $m$ is rolling without slipping on a horizontal surface. A constant force $F$ is applied to the center of mass of the cylinder, parallel to the surface. Find the acceleration of the center of mass and the angular acceleration of the cylinder.

We then introduced the equations of motion for a rigid body, including the moment of inertia and the angular momentum. We also discussed the principle of conservation of angular momentum and how it can be applied to solve problems involving rigid bodies in motion.



Next, we delved into the dynamics of a rigid body in free fall, examining the forces and torques acting on the body and how they affect its motion. We also explored the concept of rotational kinetic energy and how it relates to the motion of a rigid body. We then moved on to discuss the dynamics of a rigid body in circular motion, including the centripetal force and the concept of angular velocity.



Finally, we applied our knowledge of rigid body dynamics to solve real-world problems, such as the motion of a spinning top and the motion of a gyroscope. We also discussed the stability of a spinning body and how it can be affected by external forces.



Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. By mastering these concepts, readers will be able to apply them to a wide range of engineering and physics problems.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction:



In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. In this chapter, we will expand our understanding to two-degree-of-freedom systems, which are commonly found in engineering applications. These systems involve two independent variables that are coupled together, resulting in more complex dynamics and control challenges.



We will begin by discussing the basics of two-degree-of-freedom systems, including their mathematical representation and properties. This will include a review of concepts such as natural frequency, damping ratio, and resonance, as well as the equations of motion for these systems.



Next, we will delve into the topic of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will explore the different types of vibrations that can occur, such as free and forced vibrations, and how they are affected by the system's parameters. We will also discuss the concept of mode shapes and how they can be used to understand the behavior of these systems.



The final section of this chapter will focus on control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will discuss various control strategies, including passive and active control, and how they can be applied to these systems to improve their performance. We will also explore the concept of feedback control and how it can be used to stabilize these systems.



By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid understanding of the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. This knowledge will be essential for engineers and researchers working with these systems in various fields, such as mechanical, aerospace, and civil engineering. 





## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in the course 2.003: Modeling Dynamics and Control I. This will serve as a refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



#### 13.1a Overview of 2.003



2.003 is an introductory course on modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. It covers the fundamental principles and techniques used in the analysis and design of dynamic systems. The course is divided into three main parts: modeling, analysis, and control.



In the modeling part, students learn how to represent physical systems using mathematical models. This includes understanding the concepts of mass, stiffness, and damping, and how they relate to the behavior of a system. Students also learn how to derive the equations of motion for different types of systems, such as mechanical, electrical, and thermal systems.



The analysis part of the course focuses on understanding the behavior of dynamic systems. This includes studying the concepts of natural frequency, damping ratio, and resonance, and how they affect the response of a system. Students also learn how to analyze the stability of a system and how to determine its response to different inputs.



In the final part of the course, students learn about control techniques for dynamic systems. This includes both open-loop and closed-loop control strategies, as well as the concept of feedback control. Students also learn how to design controllers to achieve desired system performance.



Overall, 2.003 provides a solid foundation for understanding the dynamics and control of single-degree-of-freedom systems. This knowledge will be essential for understanding the more complex dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems, which we will explore in the rest of this chapter.

### Section: 13.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in the course 2.003: Modeling Dynamics and Control I. This will serve as a refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



#### 13.1a Overview of 2.003



2.003 is an introductory course on modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. It covers the fundamental principles and techniques used in the analysis and design of dynamic systems. The course is divided into three main parts: modeling, analysis, and control.



In the modeling part, students learn how to represent physical systems using mathematical models. This includes understanding the concepts of mass, stiffness, and damping, and how they relate to the behavior of a system. Students also learn how to derive the equations of motion for different types of systems, such as mechanical, electrical, and thermal systems.



The analysis part of the course focuses on understanding the behavior of dynamic systems. This includes studying the concepts of natural frequency, damping ratio, and resonance, and how they affect the response of a system. Students also learn how to analyze the stability of a system and how to determine its response to different inputs.

### Subsection: 13.1b Key Concepts from 2.003



In this subsection, we will highlight some of the key concepts covered in 2.003 that are relevant to the study of two-degree-of-freedom systems.



#### Mass, Stiffness, and Damping



In 2.003, we learned that every physical system can be represented by a mathematical model that includes three main components: mass, stiffness, and damping. These components are essential in understanding the behavior of a system and how it responds to external forces.



Mass represents the inertia of a system and is typically denoted by the symbol $m$. Stiffness, denoted by $k$, represents the resistance of a system to deformation. Damping, denoted by $c$, represents the dissipation of energy in a system. These three components work together to determine the dynamic behavior of a system.



#### Equations of Motion



One of the key skills we learned in 2.003 was how to derive the equations of motion for different types of systems. These equations describe the motion of a system and are essential in analyzing its behavior. In two-degree-of-freedom systems, we will use these equations to understand the coupled motion of two masses.



#### Natural Frequency, Damping Ratio, and Resonance



In 2.003, we also studied the concepts of natural frequency, damping ratio, and resonance. These concepts are crucial in understanding the behavior of dynamic systems. Natural frequency, denoted by $\omega_n$, is the frequency at which a system will vibrate without any external forces. Damping ratio, denoted by $\zeta$, represents the amount of damping in a system and affects its response to external forces. Resonance occurs when the frequency of an external force matches the natural frequency of a system, resulting in large amplitude vibrations.



#### Stability and Response Analysis



Another important aspect of 2.003 was learning how to analyze the stability of a system and its response to different inputs. We learned about the different types of stability, such as stable, unstable, and marginally stable, and how to determine the stability of a system using the Routh-Hurwitz stability criterion. We also learned how to analyze the response of a system to different inputs, such as step, ramp, and sinusoidal inputs.



#### Control Techniques



Finally, in 2.003, we were introduced to control techniques for dynamic systems. We learned about open-loop and closed-loop control strategies and how feedback control can be used to improve the performance of a system. We also learned how to design controllers to achieve desired system performance, such as reducing settling time or overshoot.



In the next section, we will build upon these key concepts and apply them to the study of two-degree-of-freedom systems.

# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. Now, we will extend our understanding to more complex systems with multiple degrees of freedom. In this section, we will focus on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion.



#### 13.2a Overview of Plane-motion Kinematics



Plane-motion kinematics deals with the study of the motion of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane. This is an important concept in the field of dynamics and control as many real-world systems can be approximated as two-dimensional motion. In this subsection, we will review the key concepts and equations related to plane-motion kinematics.



First, let's define some important terms. A rigid body is a body that maintains its shape and size even when subjected to external forces. In plane motion, the body is constrained to move in a two-dimensional plane, meaning that all points on the body move in parallel planes. The motion of a rigid body can be described by its position, velocity, and acceleration.



To understand the motion of a rigid body, we need to define a reference frame. A reference frame is a coordinate system that is used to measure the position, velocity, and acceleration of a body. In plane-motion kinematics, we typically use a Cartesian coordinate system with two axes, x and y, to represent the two-dimensional plane.



Now, let's discuss the equations that govern the motion of a rigid body in plane motion. The position of a point on the body can be described by its x and y coordinates, denoted as x(t) and y(t), respectively. The velocity of the point can be calculated by taking the time derivative of its position, giving us the equations:



$$

v_x(t) = \frac{dx(t)}{dt}

$$



$$

v_y(t) = \frac{dy(t)}{dt}

$$



Similarly, the acceleration of the point can be calculated by taking the time derivative of its velocity, giving us the equations:



$$

a_x(t) = \frac{dv_x(t)}{dt}

$$



$$

a_y(t) = \frac{dv_y(t)}{dt}

$$



These equations can be extended to describe the motion of the entire rigid body by considering the position, velocity, and acceleration of multiple points on the body. By analyzing the motion of different points on the body, we can gain a better understanding of its overall motion.



In the next subsection, we will apply these concepts to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion, we will be able to model and analyze the behavior of these systems more effectively.



#### 13.2b Key Equations for Plane-motion Kinematics



To summarize, the key equations for plane-motion kinematics are:



- Position: x(t), y(t)

- Velocity: v_x(t) = dx(t)/dt, v_y(t) = dy(t)/dt

- Acceleration: a_x(t) = dv_x(t)/dt, a_y(t) = dv_y(t)/dt



These equations can be used to describe the motion of a rigid body in a two-dimensional plane. By analyzing the motion of different points on the body, we can gain a better understanding of its overall motion. In the next section, we will apply these concepts to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems.

## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.2b Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous subsection, we discussed the basics of plane-motion kinematics and its importance in understanding the motion of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane. In this subsection, we will explore some of the applications of plane-motion kinematics in various fields.



#### 13.2b Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



One of the main applications of plane-motion kinematics is in the field of robotics. Robot kinematics deals with the study of the motion of robots, including motion planning, singularity avoidance, redundancy, and collision avoidance. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane, engineers can design and control robots to perform complex tasks with precision and efficiency.



Another important application of plane-motion kinematics is in the field of factory automation. Many industrial processes involve the use of machines that move in a two-dimensional plane, such as conveyor belts, robotic arms, and assembly lines. By applying the principles of plane-motion kinematics, engineers can optimize the design and control of these systems to improve productivity and efficiency in factories.



In the field of aerospace engineering, plane-motion kinematics is used to study the motion of spacecraft in orbit. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane, engineers can accurately predict and control the motion of spacecraft, ensuring their safe and efficient operation in space.



Another interesting application of plane-motion kinematics is in the field of virtual reality (VR) warehouses. VR warehouses use advanced motion tracking technology to simulate the movement of objects in a virtual environment. By applying the principles of plane-motion kinematics, engineers can accurately track and control the motion of virtual objects, creating a realistic and immersive experience for users.



In summary, plane-motion kinematics has a wide range of applications in various fields, including robotics, factory automation, aerospace engineering, and virtual reality. By understanding the principles and equations of plane-motion kinematics, engineers can design and control complex systems with precision and efficiency. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous subsection, we discussed the basics of plane-motion kinematics and its applications in various fields. However, as with any mathematical model, there are challenges that arise when applying plane-motion kinematics to real-world systems. In this subsection, we will explore some of these challenges and how engineers and scientists have addressed them.



#### 13.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



One of the main challenges in plane-motion kinematics is dealing with non-rigid bodies. While the principles of plane-motion kinematics are based on the assumption of rigid bodies, many real-world systems involve deformable bodies. This can lead to inaccuracies in the model and affect the performance of the system. To address this challenge, engineers have developed more advanced models that take into account the deformations of the body, such as the finite element method.



Another challenge in plane-motion kinematics is dealing with non-ideal conditions. In real-world systems, there are often external forces and disturbances that can affect the motion of the body. These forces may not be accounted for in the model, leading to discrepancies between the predicted and actual motion. To overcome this challenge, engineers use control theory to design controllers that can compensate for these disturbances and ensure the desired motion of the body.



In some cases, the assumptions made in plane-motion kinematics may not hold true. For example, in systems with high speeds or large deformations, the assumption of small displacements may not be valid. In these cases, more advanced models, such as nonlinear dynamics, must be used to accurately predict the motion of the body.



Another challenge in plane-motion kinematics is dealing with uncertainty. In many real-world systems, there may be uncertainties in the parameters or initial conditions of the system. This can lead to errors in the model and affect the performance of the system. To address this challenge, engineers use robust control techniques that can handle uncertainties and ensure the stability and performance of the system.



Despite these challenges, plane-motion kinematics remains a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the motion of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane. By continuously improving and refining our models, we can overcome these challenges and continue to apply plane-motion kinematics in various fields, from robotics and factory automation to aerospace engineering and virtual reality.





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of plane-motion for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems, which involves understanding the forces and torques that act on the body and how they affect its motion.



#### 13.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane-motion, we must first define the forces and torques acting on the body. These can be divided into two categories: external forces and internal forces. External forces are those that act on the body from outside, such as gravity, friction, and applied forces. Internal forces, on the other hand, are those that arise within the body, such as tension, compression, and shear.



To model the dynamics of a rigid body, we use Newton's second law, which states that the sum of all external forces acting on the body is equal to its mass times its acceleration. This can be expressed mathematically as:



$$\sum \mathbf{F}_{ext} = m\mathbf{a}$$



where $\mathbf{F}_{ext}$ is the vector sum of all external forces, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\mathbf{a}$ is its acceleration.



In addition to external forces, we must also consider the effects of torques on the body. Torques are rotational forces that cause the body to rotate about an axis. Similar to forces, we can express the sum of all external torques as:



$$\sum \mathbf{\tau}_{ext} = I\boldsymbol{\alpha}$$



where $\mathbf{\tau}_{ext}$ is the vector sum of all external torques, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body, and $\boldsymbol{\alpha}$ is its angular acceleration.



To fully understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane-motion, we must also consider the effects of internal forces and torques. These can be more complex to model, as they depend on the material properties and geometry of the body. However, by using principles of mechanics and solid mechanics, we can develop models that accurately capture the behavior of these internal forces and torques.



One of the main challenges in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is dealing with the complexity of real-world systems. As mentioned in the previous section, many systems involve non-rigid bodies, non-ideal conditions, and uncertainties. To overcome these challenges, engineers and scientists have developed advanced techniques such as finite element analysis, control theory, and nonlinear dynamics.



In conclusion, understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane-motion is crucial for accurately predicting the behavior of real-world systems. By considering external and internal forces and torques, and utilizing advanced modeling techniques, we can develop models that accurately capture the complex dynamics of these systems. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the dynamics of a rigid body in plane-motion and how external forces and torques affect its motion. Now, we will explore some applications of this concept in various fields.



#### 13.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



One of the most common applications of plane-motion dynamics is in the design and analysis of mechanical systems. By understanding the forces and torques acting on a rigid body, engineers can predict its motion and design systems that can withstand these forces.



For example, in the design of a car suspension system, engineers must consider the forces and torques acting on the wheels as the car moves over different terrains. By using the principles of plane-motion dynamics, they can design a suspension system that can absorb these forces and provide a smooth ride for the passengers.



Another application of plane-motion dynamics is in the field of robotics. By understanding the forces and torques acting on a robot arm, engineers can design control systems that can accurately move the arm to perform various tasks. This is especially important in industries such as manufacturing, where robots are used to perform repetitive and precise tasks.



In the field of aerospace engineering, plane-motion dynamics is crucial in the design and control of aircraft and spacecraft. By understanding the forces and torques acting on these vehicles, engineers can design control systems that can stabilize and maneuver them in flight.



Furthermore, plane-motion dynamics is also used in the study of biomechanics, where it is applied to understand the movement of the human body and design prosthetics and assistive devices.



In summary, the applications of plane-motion dynamics are vast and diverse, ranging from mechanical systems to robotics, aerospace engineering, and even the human body. By understanding the forces and torques acting on a rigid body, we can design and control systems that can perform various tasks and withstand external forces. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 13: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section: 13.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 13.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the dynamics of a rigid body in plane-motion and its applications in various fields. However, there are also some challenges that arise when dealing with plane-motion dynamics.



#### 13.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



One of the main challenges in plane-motion dynamics is accurately modeling the complex interactions between multiple bodies. In real-world systems, there are often multiple rigid bodies interacting with each other, and the forces and torques between them can be difficult to predict. This is especially true in systems with flexible bodies, where the dynamics become even more complex.



Another challenge is dealing with non-conservative forces, such as friction and air resistance. These forces can significantly affect the motion of a rigid body and must be carefully considered in the modeling process. In some cases, these forces may even cause instability or unexpected behavior in the system.



Additionally, the assumptions made in the modeling process can also pose challenges. For example, the assumption of rigid bodies may not hold true in all cases, and the effects of deformation and flexibility must be taken into account. Similarly, the assumption of small angles in the equations of motion may not be valid for large amplitude vibrations.



Furthermore, the accuracy of the results obtained from plane-motion dynamics is highly dependent on the accuracy of the initial conditions and parameters used in the model. Small errors in these values can lead to significant discrepancies in the predicted motion of the system.



In summary, while plane-motion dynamics is a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the motion of rigid bodies, it also presents several challenges that must be carefully considered and addressed in order to obtain accurate results. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. We began by discussing the concept of natural frequencies and mode shapes, and how they can be used to analyze the behavior of a system. We then moved on to studying the response of a two-degree-of-freedom system to external forces, and how to calculate the steady-state response using the method of complex amplitudes. We also discussed the concept of resonance and how it can be avoided through proper control design.



Next, we delved into the topic of vibration isolation, where we explored the use of passive and active control techniques to reduce the effects of vibrations on a system. We learned about the importance of damping in controlling vibrations and how to calculate the optimal damping ratio for a given system. We also discussed the use of vibration absorbers and how they can be used to reduce the amplitude of vibrations in a system.



Finally, we concluded the chapter by discussing the concept of coupled vibrations and how to model and analyze them using the method of modal analysis. We learned about the importance of orthogonality in modal analysis and how it can simplify the calculations involved. Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems, which is essential for engineers and researchers working in this field.

A two-degree-of-freedom system has a damping ratio of 0.2. Calculate the optimal damping ratio for this system.



#### Exercise 3

A two-degree-of-freedom system is subjected to an external force with a frequency of 10 Hz. If the system has a natural frequency of 8 Hz, will the system experience resonance? If so, at what frequency?



#### Exercise 4

A two-degree-of-freedom system is designed to have a natural frequency of 15 Hz. However, during testing, it is found that the actual natural frequency is 16 Hz. Calculate the percentage error in the natural frequency.



#### Exercise 5

A two-degree-of-freedom system is subjected to an external force with a frequency of 20 Hz. If the system has a natural frequency of 18 Hz, what is the damping ratio required to ensure that the system does not experience resonance?

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction



In the previous chapter, we discussed the basics of modeling dynamics and control for simple systems. We explored the concepts of state-space representation, transfer functions, and feedback control. In this chapter, we will build upon that knowledge and delve into the control of systems with rigid bodies.



Rigid bodies are objects that do not deform under the influence of external forces. They are commonly found in mechanical and aerospace systems, such as robots, satellites, and airplanes. These systems are complex and require a more advanced understanding of dynamics and control in order to effectively design and implement control strategies.



In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to the control of systems with rigid bodies. We will begin by discussing the modeling of rigid bodies, including the use of Euler angles and quaternions. We will then explore different control techniques, such as linear and nonlinear control, and their applications in controlling rigid body systems. Additionally, we will discuss the challenges and limitations of controlling these systems, such as actuator saturation and disturbances.



By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics and control of systems with rigid bodies. This knowledge will be valuable in designing and implementing control strategies for a wide range of mechanical and aerospace systems. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of controlling rigid body systems.





## Chapter 14: Control of Systems with Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 14.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in 2.003, "Dynamics and Control I". This will serve as a refresher for readers who have already taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



2.003 focuses on the fundamentals of modeling and controlling simple systems. It covers topics such as state-space representation, transfer functions, and feedback control. These concepts are essential for understanding the control of systems with rigid bodies, as they provide a foundation for more advanced techniques.



One of the key concepts covered in 2.003 is state-space representation. This is a mathematical model used to describe the behavior of a system over time. It consists of state variables, which represent the system's internal state, and input and output variables, which represent the system's inputs and outputs. State-space representation is a powerful tool for analyzing and designing control systems.



Another important concept covered in 2.003 is transfer functions. These are mathematical representations of the relationship between the input and output of a system. Transfer functions are useful for analyzing the stability and performance of a control system.



Feedback control is also a major topic in 2.003. This is a control strategy where the output of a system is measured and used to adjust the input in order to achieve a desired output. Feedback control is widely used in various industries and is an essential tool for controlling systems with rigid bodies.



Overall, 2.003 provides a solid foundation for understanding the control of systems with rigid bodies. It introduces key concepts and techniques that will be further explored in this chapter. Now, let's move on to discussing the control of systems with rigid bodies in more detail.

### Subsection: 14.1b Key Concepts from 2.003



In this subsection, we will delve deeper into some of the key concepts from 2.003 that are essential for understanding the control of systems with rigid bodies. These concepts include state-space representation, transfer functions, and feedback control.



#### State-Space Representation



As mentioned earlier, state-space representation is a mathematical model used to describe the behavior of a system over time. It consists of state variables, input variables, and output variables. The state variables represent the internal state of the system, while the input and output variables represent the system's inputs and outputs, respectively.



State-space representation is a powerful tool for analyzing and designing control systems because it allows us to model complex systems in a simple and intuitive way. It also provides a framework for understanding the behavior of a system and how it responds to different inputs.



#### Transfer Functions



Transfer functions are mathematical representations of the relationship between the input and output of a system. They are typically represented as a ratio of polynomials in the Laplace domain. Transfer functions are useful for analyzing the stability and performance of a control system.



In 2.003, you learned how to derive transfer functions from state-space models and how to use them to analyze the behavior of a system. This knowledge will be crucial for understanding the control of systems with rigid bodies, as transfer functions are commonly used in control design.



#### Feedback Control



Feedback control is a control strategy where the output of a system is measured and used to adjust the input in order to achieve a desired output. This is achieved through a feedback loop, where the output is compared to the desired output and the difference is used to adjust the input.



In 2.003, you learned about different types of feedback control, such as proportional, integral, and derivative control. These control strategies are widely used in various industries and are essential for controlling systems with rigid bodies.



Now that we have reviewed these key concepts from 2.003, we can move on to discussing the control of systems with rigid bodies in more detail. In the next section, we will explore the challenges and techniques involved in controlling systems with rigid bodies.

# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 14: Control of Systems with Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 14.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 14.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of modeling and controlling systems with rigid bodies. Now, we will dive deeper into the topic by exploring the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. This is an important aspect of understanding the behavior of rigid bodies and is essential for designing effective control strategies.



#### Kinematic width



Before we delve into the specifics of plane-motion kinematics, let's first define the concept of kinematic width. This term refers to the extent of a moving set of points in a given space. In the context of rigid bodies, it represents the range of motion of the body in a particular direction. Understanding the kinematic width is crucial for analyzing the dynamics and designing control systems for rigid bodies.



#### Plane-motion kinematics



Now, let's focus on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. This refers to the study of the motion of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane. In this case, the body is constrained to move in a single plane, making the analysis and control of its motion more manageable.



The kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion can be described using various formalisms, such as rotation formalisms and conversion from axis-angle. These provide mathematical representations of the body's motion and are useful for analyzing and designing control systems.



#### Rotation formalisms in three dimensions



One of the most commonly used rotation formalisms is the axis-angle representation. This involves representing the rotation of a rigid body in three dimensions using an axis and an angle. The axis is defined by a vector, while the angle represents the amount of rotation around that axis.



Another commonly used rotation formalism is the extended Kalman filter. This is a mathematical model used for state estimation in continuous-time systems. It involves predicting and updating the state of a system based on measurements and a given model.



#### Conversion from axis-angle



The conversion from axis-angle is a useful tool for transforming between different rotation formalisms. It involves converting the axis and angle representation to other forms, such as Euler angles or quaternions. This allows for a more comprehensive analysis of the rigid body's motion and can aid in the design of control systems.



#### Extended Kalman filter



The extended Kalman filter is a powerful tool for state estimation in continuous-time systems. It involves predicting and updating the state of a system based on measurements and a given model. This is particularly useful for controlling systems with rigid bodies, as it allows for real-time adjustments to be made based on measured data.



#### Generalizations



The extended Kalman filter can also be extended to handle more complex systems, such as continuous-time systems with discrete-time measurements. This is known as the continuous-time extended Kalman filter and involves predicting and updating the state of a system based on both continuous-time and discrete-time data.



#### Discrete-time measurements



In many real-world applications, measurements are taken at discrete intervals, even though the system itself is continuous-time. In these cases, the discrete-time extended Kalman filter is used to estimate the state of the system. This involves predicting and updating the state based on both continuous-time and discrete-time measurements, making it a more accurate and robust approach.



In conclusion, understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion is crucial for designing effective control strategies. By using various rotation formalisms and tools like the extended Kalman filter, we can accurately model and control the motion of rigid bodies in real-time. In the next section, we will explore the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion and how they can be controlled.
