### Section: 17.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in the course 2.003: Modeling Dynamics and Control I. This will serve as a refresher for readers who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



2.003 is an introductory course that covers the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control. It provides a solid foundation for understanding the principles and equations used in the field of dynamics and control. The course covers topics such as kinematics, dynamics, and control systems, and their applications in various engineering fields.



One of the key concepts covered in 2.003 is the concept of kinematics. Kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause the motion. It involves the analysis of position, velocity, and acceleration of objects in motion. In this course, we learned about the different types of motion, such as rectilinear, curvilinear, and rotational motion, and how to describe them using equations and diagrams.



Another important concept covered in 2.003 is dynamics. Dynamics is the study of motion in relation to the forces that cause it. This course introduced us to the equations of motion, which describe the relationship between an object's position, velocity, acceleration, and the forces acting on it. We also learned about the principle of superposition, which states that the total effect of multiple forces acting on an object is equal to the sum of the individual effects of each force.



In addition to kinematics and dynamics, 2.003 also covered the basics of control systems. A control system is a system that uses feedback to maintain a desired output. We learned about the different types of control systems, such as open-loop and closed-loop systems, and their applications in various engineering fields.



Overall, 2.003 provided a comprehensive understanding of the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control. It laid the groundwork for further exploration of these concepts in this course, where we will focus specifically on plane-motion kinematics. In the next section, we will provide an overview of the topics covered in this course and how they relate to the concepts learned in 2.003.

## Section: 17.1b Key Concepts from 2.003



In this subsection, we will delve deeper into some of the key concepts covered in 2.003. These concepts are essential for understanding plane-motion kinematics and will serve as a foundation for the rest of this chapter.



### Kinematics



As mentioned earlier, kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. In 2.003, we learned about the three basic types of motion: rectilinear, curvilinear, and rotational. Rectilinear motion is motion along a straight line, while curvilinear motion is motion along a curved path. Rotational motion, on the other hand, involves objects rotating around a fixed axis.



To describe these types of motion, we use equations such as the position, velocity, and acceleration equations. For example, the position equation for rectilinear motion is given by $x(t) = x_0 + v_0t + \frac{1}{2}at^2$, where $x_0$ is the initial position, $v_0$ is the initial velocity, and $a$ is the acceleration. These equations allow us to calculate the position, velocity, and acceleration of an object at any given time.



### Dynamics



Dynamics is the study of motion in relation to the forces that cause it. In 2.003, we learned about the equations of motion, which describe the relationship between an object's position, velocity, acceleration, and the forces acting on it. These equations are essential for understanding how objects move in response to different forces.



One of the key concepts in dynamics is the principle of superposition. This principle states that the total effect of multiple forces acting on an object is equal to the sum of the individual effects of each force. This allows us to break down complex systems into simpler parts and analyze them separately.



### Control Systems



Control systems are systems that use feedback to maintain a desired output. In 2.003, we learned about the different types of control systems, such as open-loop and closed-loop systems. Open-loop systems do not use feedback, while closed-loop systems do. We also learned about the concept of stability, which is crucial for designing effective control systems.

- "Control Systems Engineering" by Norman S. Nise



These resources provide additional examples, practice problems, and real-world applications of the concepts covered in 2.003.

# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous section, we provided a brief review of the concepts covered in the course 2.003: Modeling Dynamics and Control I. Now, we will dive deeper into the topic of plane-motion kinematics, specifically focusing on rigid bodies.



A rigid body is an idealized physical body that does not deform or change shape under the action of external forces. In reality, all bodies deform to some extent, but for the purpose of simplifying our analysis, we will assume that the bodies we are studying are rigid.



In plane-motion kinematics, we are interested in the motion of rigid bodies in a two-dimensional plane. This type of motion is commonly seen in engineering applications, such as in the motion of vehicles, robots, and machinery. To understand the motion of rigid bodies, we need to define some key concepts and parameters.



First, we define the position of a rigid body as the location of a reference point on the body. This reference point is often referred to as the center of mass, and it is the point around which the body rotates. The position of the body can be described using Cartesian coordinates or polar coordinates, depending on the type of motion being analyzed.



Next, we define the orientation of a rigid body as the direction in which the body is facing. This can be described using angles, such as the yaw, pitch, and roll angles, or using a rotation matrix. The orientation of the body is important in determining how it moves and interacts with its environment.



To fully understand the motion of a rigid body, we need to consider both its position and orientation. This is where the concept of kinematic chains comes into play. A kinematic chain is a series of rigid bodies connected by joints, which allows for relative motion between the bodies. By analyzing the kinematic chain, we can determine the position and orientation of each body in the chain.



In this section, we will explore the different types of motion that can occur in a plane, such as translation, rotation, and general plane motion. We will also discuss the equations and diagrams used to describe these types of motion, as well as the concept of relative motion and how it applies to rigid bodies.



By the end of this section, readers will have a solid understanding of the kinematics of rigid bodies in a plane, which will serve as a foundation for the more complex topics covered in the following sections. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.2b Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous section, we discussed the basic concepts of plane-motion kinematics for rigid bodies. Now, we will explore some of the practical applications of this topic.



One of the most common applications of plane-motion kinematics is in the field of factory automation. In this context, kinematic chains are used to model the movement of robotic arms and other machinery. By understanding the kinematics of these systems, engineers can design more efficient and precise automation processes.



Another important application of plane-motion kinematics is in the field of spacecraft dynamics. Poinsot's construction, also known as the Poinsot's ellipsoid, is a visualization tool used to understand the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit. This construction is based on the principles of plane-motion kinematics and is essential for spacecraft control and navigation.



In addition to these specific applications, the concepts of plane-motion kinematics are also used in a variety of other fields. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is the maximum distance between two points on a moving body, is used in computer graphics and animation to create realistic movements.



For those interested in further reading on this topic, the extended Kalman filter is a useful tool for estimating the state of a system based on noisy measurements. This filter is based on the principles of plane-motion kinematics and is commonly used in control systems and robotics.



Finally, it is worth noting that the continuous-time extended Kalman filter, unlike its discrete-time counterpart, has coupled prediction and update steps. This means that the prediction step is affected by the update step, making it a more complex and challenging tool to use.



In summary, plane-motion kinematics is a fundamental topic in the study of dynamics and control. Its applications are vast and diverse, ranging from factory automation to spacecraft dynamics. By understanding the principles of plane-motion kinematics, engineers and scientists can design and analyze complex systems with precision and accuracy. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



In the previous section, we discussed the basic concepts and applications of plane-motion kinematics for rigid bodies. However, as with any mathematical model, there are certain challenges that arise when applying these concepts to real-world systems. In this section, we will explore some of these challenges and how they can be addressed.



One of the main challenges in plane-motion kinematics is dealing with non-rigid bodies. While the concepts we have discussed so far assume that the body is rigid, in reality, most objects have some degree of flexibility. This can lead to errors in the kinematic model and affect the accuracy of predictions. To address this challenge, advanced techniques such as finite element analysis can be used to model the flexibility of the body and incorporate it into the kinematic equations.



Another challenge is dealing with non-uniform motion. So far, we have assumed that the motion of the body is uniform, meaning that all points on the body move with the same velocity and acceleration. However, in many real-world systems, this is not the case. For example, in a robotic arm, different joints may have different velocities and accelerations. To account for this, we can use the concept of relative motion, where the motion of each point is described relative to a chosen reference point.



In addition to these challenges, there are also limitations in the assumptions made in plane-motion kinematics. For example, we have assumed that the body is moving in a two-dimensional plane, but in reality, many systems have three-dimensional motion. In these cases, more advanced kinematic models, such as spatial kinematics, must be used.



Furthermore, the accuracy of the kinematic model is highly dependent on the accuracy of the measurements used to determine the motion of the body. In real-world systems, there may be noise and errors in these measurements, which can lead to inaccuracies in the kinematic model. To address this, advanced filtering techniques, such as the extended Kalman filter mentioned in the previous section, can be used to estimate the true state of the system based on noisy measurements.



In summary, while plane-motion kinematics provides a powerful tool for modeling the motion of rigid bodies, there are several challenges that must be considered when applying these concepts to real-world systems. By understanding and addressing these challenges, we can create more accurate and reliable kinematic models for a wide range of applications.





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of plane motion for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems, which involves understanding the forces and torques acting on the body and how they affect its motion.



#### Forces and Torques



In order to understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we must first consider the forces and torques acting on the body. These forces and torques can be divided into two categories: external and internal.



External forces and torques are those that act on the body from outside sources, such as gravity, friction, or applied forces. These forces and torques can be represented by vectors, with the direction and magnitude determined by the properties of the body and the external environment.



Internal forces and torques, on the other hand, are those that act between different parts of the body. These forces and torques are a result of the body's structure and can be more difficult to determine. However, they are crucial in understanding the overall dynamics of the system.



#### Equations of Motion



Once we have identified the external and internal forces and torques acting on the body, we can use Newton's laws of motion to derive the equations of motion for the system. These equations describe the relationship between the forces and torques acting on the body and its resulting motion.



In plane motion, we can use the equations of motion to determine the translational and rotational motion of the body. This involves solving for the acceleration, velocity, and position of the body's center of mass, as well as the angular acceleration, angular velocity, and orientation of the body.



#### Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



Similar to the challenges faced in plane-motion kinematics, there are also challenges in applying the concepts of plane-motion dynamics to real-world systems. One of the main challenges is dealing with non-rigid bodies, as the assumptions made for rigid bodies may not hold true. In these cases, more advanced techniques, such as finite element analysis, must be used to accurately model the flexibility of the body.



Another challenge is dealing with non-uniform motion, where different points on the body may have different velocities and accelerations. To address this, we can use the concept of relative motion, as discussed in the previous section.



Furthermore, the assumptions made in plane-motion dynamics, such as the body moving in a two-dimensional plane, may not hold true for all systems. In these cases, more advanced models, such as spatial dynamics, must be used.



#### Conclusion



In this section, we have explored the basics of plane-motion dynamics for rigid bodies. By understanding the forces and torques acting on the body and using the equations of motion, we can accurately predict the motion of the system. However, it is important to keep in mind the challenges and limitations that may arise when applying these concepts to real-world systems. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the equations of motion for a rigid body in plane motion. Now, we will explore some applications of these dynamics in various fields.



#### Aerospace Engineering



One of the most prominent applications of plane-motion dynamics is in the field of aerospace engineering. The study of aircraft and spacecraft dynamics heavily relies on the principles of plane motion, as these systems often involve rigid bodies moving in two dimensions.



For example, in the design and control of an aircraft, understanding the forces and torques acting on the body is crucial in determining its stability and maneuverability. The equations of motion for plane motion can be used to model the behavior of an aircraft in flight and aid in the design of control systems to ensure safe and efficient operation.



#### Robotics



Another field that heavily relies on plane-motion dynamics is robotics. Robots often involve rigid bodies moving in two dimensions, and the principles of plane motion are essential in understanding their behavior and designing control systems for them.



For instance, in the design of a robotic arm, the equations of motion for plane motion can be used to determine the forces and torques required to move the arm to a desired position and orientation. This information is crucial in designing the motors and actuators needed to control the arm's motion accurately.



#### Biomechanics



The study of human and animal movement, known as biomechanics, also utilizes plane-motion dynamics. By understanding the forces and torques acting on the body during different movements, researchers can gain insights into the mechanics of human and animal locomotion.



For example, in sports science, biomechanics is used to analyze the movements of athletes and improve their performance. By studying the forces and torques involved in different sports movements, coaches and trainers can develop training programs to optimize an athlete's performance.



#### Conclusion



In conclusion, the principles of plane-motion dynamics have a wide range of applications in various fields, including aerospace engineering, robotics, and biomechanics. By understanding the forces and torques acting on a rigid body in plane motion, we can gain insights into the behavior of complex systems and design control systems to optimize their performance. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 17: Plane-motion Kinematics:



### Section: 17.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Subsection: 17.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



In the previous section, we discussed the equations of motion for a rigid body in plane motion and explored some applications of these dynamics in various fields. However, as with any mathematical model, there are challenges and limitations that must be considered when using plane-motion dynamics.



#### Nonlinear Dynamics



One of the main challenges in plane-motion dynamics is dealing with nonlinear systems. In many real-world scenarios, the equations of motion for a rigid body in plane motion are nonlinear, making them difficult to solve analytically. This can lead to the need for numerical methods and approximations, which may introduce errors and limitations in the model.



#### Complex Systems



Another challenge in plane-motion dynamics is dealing with complex systems. In some cases, the rigid body may have multiple degrees of freedom, making the equations of motion more complicated and difficult to solve. Additionally, the presence of external forces and torques, such as friction and air resistance, can further complicate the dynamics of the system.



#### Assumptions and Simplifications



In order to make the equations of motion more manageable, certain assumptions and simplifications are often made. For example, the rigid body may be assumed to be perfectly rigid, neglecting any deformations or flexibility. This can lead to inaccuracies in the model, especially in systems where flexibility plays a significant role.



#### Experimental Validation



Finally, it is important to note that the equations of motion for a rigid body in plane motion are based on theoretical models and assumptions. In order to accurately predict the behavior of a real-world system, experimental validation is necessary. This can involve collecting data and comparing it to the model's predictions, or using advanced techniques such as system identification to refine the model.



Despite these challenges, plane-motion dynamics remains a powerful tool in understanding and predicting the behavior of rigid bodies in two dimensions. By carefully considering the limitations and continuously improving the models through experimental validation, we can continue to advance our understanding of complex systems and their dynamics.

We began by defining the basic terms and concepts related to plane-motion kinematics, such as position, velocity, and acceleration. We then delved into the different types of motion, including rectilinear, curvilinear, and rotational motion, and discussed their corresponding kinematic equations. We also explored the concept of relative motion and how it can be used to analyze the motion of a body in relation to a reference frame.



Furthermore, we discussed the importance of understanding plane-motion kinematics in the field of engineering, particularly in the design and control of mechanical systems. By accurately modeling the motion of a system, we can predict its behavior and make informed decisions to optimize its performance. We also highlighted the significance of using mathematical tools, such as vectors and coordinate systems, to simplify and solve complex kinematic problems.



Overall, this chapter has provided a solid foundation for understanding plane-motion kinematics and its role in modeling dynamics and control. By mastering these concepts, readers will be better equipped to tackle more advanced topics in the field of engineering.

# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.1 Uniaxial Motion:



In this section, we will briefly review the concept of uniaxial motion, which is a fundamental concept in the study of dynamics and control. Uniaxial motion refers to the motion of an object along a single axis, or in other words, motion in one direction. This type of motion is commonly seen in many engineering systems, such as a car moving along a straight road or a pendulum swinging back and forth.



To understand uniaxial motion, we must first understand the concept of rotation formalisms in three dimensions. This involves converting from axis-angle representation to other forms, such as quaternions or dual quaternions. These formalisms are essential for describing the orientation and motion of objects in three-dimensional space.



One important aspect of uniaxial motion is rational motion, which involves the use of rational Bézier and B-spline motions. These are mathematical techniques used to model the motion of objects in a smooth and efficient manner. Rational Bézier curves are defined by a set of unit dual quaternions and dual weights, while B-spline curves are defined by B-spline basis functions.



To better understand uniaxial motion, we must also understand the concept of homogeneous coordinates. These are used to represent points in three-dimensional space and are crucial for describing the transformation of objects in motion. The transformation equation for uniaxial motion in terms of quaternions is given by:



$$

\tilde {\textbf{P}} = \textbf{Q}\textbf{P}\textbf{Q}^\ast + P_4 [(\textbf{Q}^0)\textbf{Q}^\ast - \textbf{Q}(\textbf{Q}^0)^\ast],

$$



where $\textbf{Q}$ and $\textbf{Q}^0$ are quaternions, and $\textbf{P}$ is the point being transformed. This equation shows how the point $\textbf{P}$ is transformed by the quaternion $\textbf{Q}$ and its conjugate $\textbf{Q}^\ast$.



In summary, uniaxial motion is a fundamental concept in the study of dynamics and control. It involves the use of rotation formalisms, rational motion techniques, and homogeneous coordinates to model the motion of objects in one direction. Understanding uniaxial motion is crucial for understanding more complex systems and designing control systems to regulate them. In the next section, we will dive deeper into the understanding of uniaxial motion by exploring its applications in the field of engineering.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.1 Uniaxial Motion:



In this section, we will continue our discussion on uniaxial motion and its applications in dynamics and control. We will also explore some additional concepts related to uniaxial motion, such as Poinsot's ellipsoid and kinetic width.



#### 18.1a Poinsot's Ellipsoid



One of the applications of uniaxial motion is in visualizing the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit. This is where Poinsot's ellipsoid comes into play. Poinsot's ellipsoid is a geometric construction that helps us understand the rotation of a rigid body in three-dimensional space. It is defined by the principal moments of inertia of the body and represents the inertia ellipsoid of the body.



The ellipsoid is named after the French mathematician Louis Poinsot, who first introduced it in 1834. It is a useful tool for visualizing the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit and can also be used to analyze the stability of the spacecraft's motion.



#### 18.1b Applications of Uniaxial Motion



Uniaxial motion has many practical applications in engineering systems. One such application is in the design and control of robotic arms. Robotic arms often have multiple joints that allow them to move in multiple directions. However, the motion of each joint can be simplified to uniaxial motion, making it easier to model and control the arm's overall motion.



Another application of uniaxial motion is in the design of suspension systems for vehicles. The motion of a vehicle's suspension can be modeled as uniaxial motion, allowing engineers to optimize the suspension's performance and stability.



#### 18.1c Kinetic Width



Kinetic width is another important concept related to uniaxial motion. It is defined as the maximum distance between two points on a rigid body that is undergoing uniaxial motion. In other words, it represents the maximum extent of the body's motion in a single direction.



Understanding the kinetic width of a body is crucial in designing and controlling its motion. It helps engineers determine the body's stability and the amount of force required to move it in a particular direction.



## Further reading



For a more in-depth understanding of uniaxial motion and its applications, we recommend the following resources:



- P. K. Agarwal, L. J. Guibas, J. Hershberger, and E. Verach. Maintaining the extent of a moving set of points.

- J. J. Craig. Introduction to Robotics: Mechanics and Control.

- R. Featherstone. Rigid Body Dynamics Algorithms.

- J. J. Uicker, G. R. Pennock, and J. E. Shigley. Theory of Machines and Mechanisms.



In the next section, we will explore another important concept related to uniaxial motion - rational motion. We will discuss the use of rational Bézier and B-spline motions in modeling the motion of objects in a smooth and efficient manner.

# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.2 Fixed-axis Rotation:



In this section, we will continue our discussion on rotation formalisms in three dimensions, specifically focusing on fixed-axis rotation. We will explore the conversion from axis-angle representation to rotation matrices, as well as alternate methods for computing rotation matrices.



#### 18.2a Understanding Fixed-axis Rotation



Fixed-axis rotation is a commonly used method for representing the rotation of a rigid body in three-dimensional space. It involves rotating the body around a fixed axis, which can be defined by a unit vector <math>{a} = [{X}, {Y}, {Z}]</math>. This axis is normalized to have a magnitude of 1, making it easier to work with mathematically.



To convert from axis-angle representation to a rotation matrix, we can use the following formula:



$$

R = \begin{bmatrix}

\cos \theta + a_x^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) - a_z \sin \theta & a_y \sin \theta + a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) \\

a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) + a_z \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_y^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_x \sin \theta \\

a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_y \sin \theta & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) + a_x \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_z^{2} (1-\cos \theta)

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from rotating the vectors (1,0,0), (0,1,0), and (0,0,1) and reducing constants. It is important to note that the angle of rotation, <math>{\theta}</math>, is equal to the magnitude of the input axis, <math>{a}</math>.



Alternatively, we can use the following formula to compute the rotation matrix:



$$

R = \begin{bmatrix}

1 - 2 q_y^2 - 2 q_z^2 & 2(q_x q_y - q_z q_r) & 2(q_x q_z + q_y q_r)\\

2(q_x q_y + q_z q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2q_z^2 & 2(q_y q_z - q_x q_r)\\

2(q_x q_z - q_y q_r) & 2(q_y q_z + q_x q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2 q_y^2

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from converting the input axis, <math>{Q}=[{X}, {Y}, {Z}]</math>, to an angle-axis representation, <math>{\theta} = \|Q\|</math>, and <math>[x,y,z] = \frac {Q} {\|Q\|}</math>. It is important to note that the input axis must be normalized before using this formula.



Fixed-axis rotation also has applications in vector rotation. We can rotate a vector, <math>{v} = (X, Y, Z)</math>, around the rotation axis, <math>{Q} = (X,Y,Z)</math>, using the following formula:



$$

v' = \begin{bmatrix}

\cos \theta & -\sin \theta & 0 \\

\sin \theta & \cos \theta & 0 \\

0 & 0 & 1

\end{bmatrix} v

$$



This formula involves calculating the cosine of the angle times the vector to rotate, plus the sine of the angle times the axis of rotation, plus one minus the cosine of the angle times the dot product of the vector and rotation axis times the axis of rotation. The dot product includes the cosine of the angle between the vector being rotated and the axis of rotation times the length of <math>{v}</math>, while the cross product includes the sine of the angle between the vector being rotated and the axis of rotation.



In conclusion, fixed-axis rotation is a useful tool for representing the rotation of a rigid body in three-dimensional space. It has various applications in engineering systems, such as robotic arms and suspension systems for vehicles. By understanding the conversion from axis-angle representation to rotation matrices and alternate methods for computing rotation matrices, we can effectively model and control the motion of a rigid body.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.2 Fixed-axis Rotation:



In this section, we will continue our discussion on rotation formalisms in three dimensions, specifically focusing on fixed-axis rotation. We will explore the conversion from axis-angle representation to rotation matrices, as well as alternate methods for computing rotation matrices.

$$

\cos \theta + a_x^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) - a_z \sin \theta & a_y \sin \theta + a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) \\

a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) + a_z \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_y^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_x \sin \theta \\

a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_y \sin \theta & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) + a_x \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_z^{2} (1-\cos \theta)

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from rotating the vectors (1,0,0), (0,1,0), and (0,0,1) and reducing constants. It is important to note that the angle of rotation, <math>{\theta}</math>, is equal to the magnitude of the input axis, <math>{a}</math>.



Alternatively, we can use the following formula to compute the rotation matrix:



$$

R = \begin{bmatrix}

1 - 2 q_y^2 - 2 q_z^2 & 2(q_x q_y - q_z q_r) & 2(q_x q_z + q_y q_r)\\

2(q_x q_y + q_z q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2q_z^2 & 2(q_y q_z - q_x q_r)\\

2(q_x q_z - q_y q_r) & 2(q_y q_z + q_x q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2 q_y^2

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from converting the input axis, <math>{Q}=[{X}, {Y}, {Z}]</math>, to an angle-axis representation. The rotation matrix is then calculated using the cosine of the angle times the vector to rotate, plus the sine of the angle times the axis of rotation, plus one minus the cosine of the angle times the dot product of the vector and rotation axis times the axis of rotation.



#### 18.2b Applications of Fixed-axis Rotation



Fixed-axis rotation has many practical applications in engineering and physics. One of the most common applications is in visualizing the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit. By using fixed-axis rotation, we can easily track the orientation of the spacecraft as it moves through space.



Another application is in line integral convolution, a technique used to visualize vector fields. By using fixed-axis rotation, we can rotate the vector field and observe how it changes over time, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of the system.



In addition, fixed-axis rotation is also used in basis matrix computation. This involves rotating the vectors (1,0,0), (0,1,0), and (0,0,1) and reducing constants to compute the resulting matrix. This matrix can then be used in various control and dynamics calculations.



Overall, fixed-axis rotation is a powerful tool for understanding and analyzing the dynamics of systems in three-dimensional space. Its applications are vast and continue to be utilized in various fields of study.

### Section: 18.2 Fixed-axis Rotation:



In this section, we will continue our discussion on rotation formalisms in three dimensions, specifically focusing on fixed-axis rotation. We will explore the conversion from axis-angle representation to rotation matrices, as well as alternate methods for computing rotation matrices.



#### 18.2a Understanding Fixed-axis Rotation

It involves rotating the body around a fixed axis, which can be defined by a unit vector <math>{a} = [{X}, {Y}, {Z}]</math>. $$

R = \begin{bmatrix}

\cos \theta + a_x^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) - a_z \sin \theta & a_y \sin \theta + a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) \\

a_x a_y (1-\cos \theta) + a_z \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_y^2 (1-\cos \theta) & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_x \sin \theta \\

a_x a_z (1-\cos \theta) - a_y \sin \theta & a_y a_z (1-\cos \theta) + a_x \sin \theta & \cos \theta + a_z^{2} (1-\cos \theta)

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from rotating the vectors (1,0,0), (0,1,0), and (0,0,1) and reducing constants. It is important to note that the angle of rotation, <math>{\theta}</math>, is equal to the magnitude of the input axis, <math>{a}</math>.



Alternatively, we can use the following formula to compute the rotation matrix:



$$

R = \begin{bmatrix}

1 - 2 q_y^2 - 2 q_z^2 & 2(q_x q_y - q_z q_r) & 2(q_x q_z + q_y q_r)\\

2(q_x q_y + q_z q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2q_z^2 & 2(q_y q_z - q_x q_r)\\

2(q_x q_z - q_y q_r) & 2(q_y q_z + q_x q_r) & 1 - 2 q_x^2 - 2 q_y^2

\end{bmatrix}

$$



This formula is derived from converting the input axis, <math>{Q}=[{X}, {Y}, {Z}]</math>, to an angle-axis representation. The rotation matrix can then be computed using the quaternion elements <math>{q_x, q_y, q_z, q_r}</math>. This method is commonly used in computer graphics and robotics.



#### 18.2b Applications of Fixed-axis Rotation



Fixed-axis rotation has various applications in engineering and physics. One of the most common applications is in visualizing the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit. By using fixed-axis rotation, we can easily track the orientation of the spacecraft as it moves through space.



Another application is in the design and control of aircraft. Fixed-axis rotation is used to model the dynamics of aircraft motion, allowing engineers to predict and control the behavior of the aircraft in flight.



#### 18.2c Challenges in Fixed-axis Rotation



While fixed-axis rotation is a useful tool for modeling dynamics and control, it does have its limitations. One of the main challenges is dealing with singularities, where the rotation matrix becomes undefined. This can occur when the rotation axis is aligned with one of the coordinate axes, resulting in a division by zero in the rotation matrix formula.



Another challenge is the difficulty in visualizing rotations in higher dimensions. In three dimensions, we can easily visualize a rotation around a fixed axis, but in higher dimensions, there are multiple axis-planes that are left invariant by the rotation. This makes it more challenging to understand and visualize the rotation.



Despite these challenges, fixed-axis rotation remains a valuable tool in modeling dynamics and control, and with advancements in technology and visualization techniques, we can continue to overcome these challenges and gain a deeper understanding of rotational motion in higher dimensions.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will review the concepts of first and second order systems, which are essential in understanding the dynamics and control of physical systems. We will also discuss the differences between these two types of systems and their applications.



#### 18.3a Understanding First Order Systems



A first order system is a mathematical model that describes the behavior of a system with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. These systems are characterized by a first order differential equation, which relates the input and output of the system. The most common example of a first order system is the RC circuit, which consists of a resistor and a capacitor connected in series.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the RC circuit. The input to this system is the voltage across the resistor, and the output is the voltage across the capacitor. The behavior of the system is described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_i - V_c)

$$



where <math>{V_c}</math> is the voltage across the capacitor, <math>{V_i}</math> is the input voltage, and <math>{R}</math> and <math>{C}</math> are the resistance and capacitance of the circuit, respectively.



One important characteristic of first order systems is their time constant, which is defined as the time it takes for the system to reach 63.2% of its steady-state value. In the case of the RC circuit, the time constant is given by <math>{\tau = RC}</math>. This parameter is crucial in understanding the transient response of the system.



First order systems have a wide range of applications in engineering, including electrical circuits, mechanical systems, and chemical processes. They are also used in control systems to model the dynamics of physical systems and design controllers to regulate their behavior.



In conclusion, first order systems are an essential concept in modeling the dynamics and control of physical systems. They are characterized by a first order differential equation and have a time constant that determines their transient response. Understanding these systems is crucial for engineers and scientists working in various fields.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will review the concepts of first and second order systems, which are essential in understanding the dynamics and control of physical systems. We will also discuss the differences between these two types of systems and their applications.



#### 18.3a Understanding First Order Systems



A first order system is a mathematical model that describes the behavior of a system with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. These systems are characterized by a first order differential equation, which relates the input and output of the system. The most common example of a first order system is the RC circuit, which consists of a resistor and a capacitor connected in series.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the RC circuit. The input to this system is the voltage across the resistor, and the output is the voltage across the capacitor. The behavior of the system is described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_i - V_c)

$$



where <math>{V_c}</math> is the voltage across the capacitor, <math>{V_i}</math> is the input voltage, and <math>{R}</math> and <math>{C}</math> are the resistance and capacitance of the circuit, respectively.



One important characteristic of first order systems is their time constant, which is defined as the time it takes for the system to reach 63.2% of its steady-state value. In the case of the RC circuit, the time constant is given by <math>{\tau = RC}</math>. This parameter is crucial in understanding the transient response of the system.



First order systems have a wide range of applications in engineering, including electrical circuits, mechanical systems, and chemical processes. They are also used in control systems to model the dynamics of physical systems and design controllers to regulate their behavior. In the field of control theory, first order systems are often used as building blocks for more complex systems, as they provide a simple and intuitive way to understand the behavior of a system.



#### 18.3b Understanding Second Order Systems



While first order systems are useful for modeling systems with a single energy storage element, many physical systems require a more complex model. Second order systems, on the other hand, are able to capture the dynamics of systems with two energy storage elements, such as a mass-spring-damper system.



The behavior of a second order system is described by a second order differential equation, which relates the input and output of the system. The most common example of a second order system is the mass-spring-damper system, which consists of a mass connected to a spring and a damper.



To better understand second order systems, let's consider the mass-spring-damper system. The input to this system is the force applied to the mass, and the output is the position of the mass. The behavior of the system is described by the following second order differential equation:



$$

m\ddot{x} + c\dot{x} + kx = F

$$



where <math>{m}</math> is the mass, <math>{c}</math> is the damping coefficient, <math>{k}</math> is the spring constant, and <math>{F}</math> is the applied force.



Similar to first order systems, second order systems also have a time constant, which is defined as the time it takes for the system to reach 63.2% of its steady-state value. However, in the case of second order systems, the time constant is given by <math>{\tau = \frac{1}{\zeta\omega_n}}</math>, where <math>{\zeta}</math> is the damping ratio and <math>{\omega_n}</math> is the natural frequency of the system.



Second order systems have a wide range of applications in engineering, including mechanical systems, electrical circuits, and control systems. They are particularly useful in control systems, as they can accurately model the dynamics of many physical systems and allow for the design of effective controllers.



In conclusion, first and second order systems are fundamental concepts in the study of dynamics and control. While first order systems are simpler and easier to understand, second order systems are able to capture the dynamics of more complex systems. Both types of systems have important applications in engineering and are essential for understanding and designing control systems.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



### Section: 18.3 First and Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will review the concepts of first and second order systems, which are essential in understanding the dynamics and control of physical systems. We will also discuss the differences between these two types of systems and their applications.



#### 18.3a Understanding First Order Systems



A first order system is a mathematical model that describes the behavior of a system with a single energy storage element, such as a capacitor or an inductor. These systems are characterized by a first order differential equation, which relates the input and output of the system. The most common example of a first order system is the RC circuit, which consists of a resistor and a capacitor connected in series.



To better understand first order systems, let's consider the RC circuit. The input to this system is the voltage across the resistor, and the output is the voltage across the capacitor. The behavior of the system is described by the following first order differential equation:



$$

\frac{dV_c}{dt} = \frac{1}{RC}(V_i - V_c)

$$



where <math>{V_c}</math> is the voltage across the capacitor, <math>{V_i}</math> is the input voltage, and <math>{R}</math> and <math>{C}</math> are the resistance and capacitance of the circuit, respectively.



One important characteristic of first order systems is their time constant, which is defined as the time it takes for the system to reach 63.2% of its steady-state value. In the case of the RC circuit, the time constant is given by <math>{\tau = RC}</math>. This parameter is crucial in understanding the transient response of the system.



First order systems have a wide range of applications in engineering, including electrical circuits, mechanical systems, and chemical processes. They are also used in control systems to model the dynamics of physical systems and design controllers to regulate their behavior. In fact, first order systems are the building blocks of more complex systems and are often used to approximate the behavior of higher order systems.



#### 18.3b Understanding Second Order Systems



Unlike first order systems, second order systems have two energy storage elements and are characterized by a second order differential equation. These systems are commonly found in mechanical systems, such as mass-spring-damper systems, and electrical systems, such as RLC circuits.



To illustrate the behavior of a second order system, let's consider the mass-spring-damper system. The input to this system is the force applied to the mass, and the output is the displacement of the mass. The behavior of the system is described by the following second order differential equation:



$$

m\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} + c\frac{dx}{dt} + kx = F

$$



where <math>{m}</math> is the mass, <math>{c}</math> is the damping coefficient, <math>{k}</math> is the spring constant, and <math>{F}</math> is the applied force.



Similar to first order systems, second order systems also have a time constant, but in this case, it is given by <math>{\tau = \frac{c}{2m}}</math>. This parameter is important in understanding the damping of the system and its response to external forces.



Second order systems have a wide range of applications in engineering, including control systems, signal processing, and vibration analysis. They are also used to model complex physical systems, such as vehicles, aircraft, and robots.



### 18.3c Comparing First and Second Order Systems



Now that we have a basic understanding of first and second order systems, let's compare the two. One key difference between these two types of systems is their order of complexity. First order systems have a single energy storage element and are described by a first order differential equation, while second order systems have two energy storage elements and are described by a second order differential equation. This means that second order systems are more complex and require more parameters to fully describe their behavior.



Another difference is the response time of the systems. First order systems have a faster response time compared to second order systems, as they have a shorter time constant. This means that first order systems reach their steady-state value quicker than second order systems.



In terms of applications, first order systems are often used to approximate the behavior of higher order systems, while second order systems are used to model more complex physical systems. Additionally, first order systems are commonly used in control systems, while second order systems have a wider range of applications, including signal processing and vibration analysis.



In conclusion, first and second order systems are both important in understanding the dynamics and control of physical systems. While they have some similarities, they also have distinct differences in terms of complexity, response time, and applications. It is important to have a solid understanding of both types of systems in order to effectively model and control physical systems.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction



In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. In this chapter, we will expand our understanding to more complex systems by delving into the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. This topic is crucial in the field of engineering as it allows us to analyze and design systems that involve multiple interconnected bodies, such as robots, vehicles, and machines.



We will begin by discussing the basic principles of rigid body dynamics, including the concepts of mass, inertia, and moment of inertia. We will then move on to studying the equations of motion for rigid bodies in plane motion, which involve both translational and rotational motion. These equations will be derived using the principles of Newton's laws and Euler's equations.



Next, we will explore the concept of angular momentum and its conservation in rigid body motion. This will provide us with a powerful tool for analyzing and predicting the behavior of complex systems. We will also discuss the effects of external forces and moments on the motion of rigid bodies, and how they can be controlled through the use of feedback control systems.



Finally, we will apply our knowledge of rigid body dynamics to practical examples, such as the motion of a spinning top and the stability of a rotating spacecraft. Through these examples, we will gain a deeper understanding of the concepts and equations presented in this chapter.



By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid foundation in the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion, which will serve as a basis for further exploration into more complex systems in the field of engineering. So let's dive in and expand our understanding of modeling dynamics and control!

# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will build upon the concepts covered in 2.003 and apply them to the more complex dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. We will begin by reviewing the fundamental principles of dynamics and then delve into the equations of motion for rigid bodies.



#### 19.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



Before we can fully understand the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion, we must first review the kinematics of rigid bodies. In 2.003, we learned that the motion of a rigid body can be described by the translation and rotation of its center of mass. This translation and rotation can be represented by the position and orientation of the body in space.



To describe the position and orientation of a rigid body, we use a set of coordinates known as the body-fixed frame. This frame is attached to the body and moves with it, allowing us to track the position and orientation of the body as it moves through space.



The motion of a rigid body can be broken down into two components: translational motion and rotational motion. Translational motion is the movement of the body's center of mass in a straight line, while rotational motion is the rotation of the body about its center of mass.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body, we use a combination of translation and rotation matrices. These matrices allow us to track the position and orientation of the body at any given time.



In addition to translation and rotation, we must also consider the velocity and acceleration of a rigid body. The velocity of a rigid body is the rate of change of its position, while the acceleration is the rate of change of its velocity. These quantities can be described using the same translation and rotation matrices used to describe the position and orientation of the body.



By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies, we can now move on to the more complex dynamics of plane motion. In the next section, we will explore the equations of motion for rigid bodies in plane motion and how they can be used to analyze and control the behavior of these systems.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will continue our exploration of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. In the previous section, we reviewed the fundamental principles of dynamics and introduced the concept of kinematics for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve deeper into the equations of motion for rigid bodies and explore their applications.



#### 19.2b Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



The kinematics of rigid bodies have many practical applications in engineering and physics. One of the most common applications is in the design and control of robotic systems. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies, engineers can design robots that can move and manipulate objects with precision and accuracy.



Another important application is in the field of aerospace engineering. The motion of spacecraft and satellites in orbit can be described using the same principles of kinematics that we have discussed. This allows engineers to accurately predict and control the motion of these complex systems.



One of the most useful tools for visualizing the motion of a rigid body is Poinsot's construction, also known as Poinsot's ellipsoid. This construction allows us to visualize the rotation of a rigid body in three-dimensional space by representing its motion as an ellipsoid. This visualization is particularly useful in understanding the complex motion of spacecraft and satellites in orbit.



In addition to these practical applications, the kinematics of rigid bodies also have important theoretical implications. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is the maximum distance between two points on a rigid body, is closely related to Poinsot's construction and can be used to analyze the stability of a rotating body.

- J. J. Craig. Introduction to Robotics: Mechanics and Control.

- R. Murray, Z. Li, and S. Sastry. A Mathematical Introduction to Robotic Manipulation.



## Generalizations



The concepts of kinematics and dynamics that we have discussed in this chapter can be extended to more complex systems. One such extension is the continuous-time extended Kalman filter, which is used for state estimation in systems with continuous-time models and discrete-time measurements.



### Continuous-time extended Kalman filter



The continuous-time extended Kalman filter is a powerful tool for estimating the state of a system with continuous-time dynamics and discrete-time measurements. It is an extension of the discrete-time extended Kalman filter, which is commonly used for state estimation in systems with discrete-time dynamics and measurements.



The model for the continuous-time extended Kalman filter is given by:



$$

\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) = f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) \quad \mathbf{w}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr)

$$



$$

\mathbf{z}(t) = h\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{v}(t) \quad \mathbf{v}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}(t)\bigr)

$$



The continuous-time extended Kalman filter follows a predict-update cycle, similar to the discrete-time extended Kalman filter. The steps for this cycle are:



1. Initialize the state estimate and covariance matrix at time $t_0$:

$$

\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t_0) = E\bigl[\mathbf{x}(t_0)\bigr] \quad \mathbf{P}(t_0) = Var\bigl[\mathbf{x}(t_0)\bigr]

$$



2. Predict the state and covariance at time $t$:

$$

\dot{\hat{\mathbf{x}}}(t) = f\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)\bigr)+\mathbf{K}(t)\Bigl(\mathbf{z}(t)-h\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)\bigr)\Bigr)

$$



$$

\dot{\mathbf{P}}(t) = \mathbf{F}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{F}(t)^{T}-\mathbf{K}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{Q}(t)

$$



3. Update the state and covariance at time $t$:

$$

\mathbf{K}(t) = \mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)^{T}\mathbf{R}(t)^{-1}

$$



$$

\mathbf{F}(t) = \left . \frac{\partial f}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)}

$$



$$

\mathbf{H}(t) = \left . \frac{\partial h}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)}

$$



Unlike the discrete-time extended Kalman filter, the prediction and update steps are coupled in the continuous-time extended Kalman filter.



#### Discrete-time measurements



In many practical applications, measurements are taken at discrete time intervals, even though the system is modeled using continuous-time dynamics. In these cases, the system model and measurement model are given by:



$$

\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) = f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) \quad \mathbf{w}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr)

$$



$$

\mathbf{z}(t) = h\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{v}(t) \quad \mathbf{v}(t) \sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}(t)\bigr)

$$



However, the prediction and update steps for the continuous-time extended Kalman filter remain the same. This is because the discrete-time measurements can be interpolated to obtain continuous-time measurements, which can then be used in the prediction and update steps.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.2 Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will continue our exploration of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. In the previous section, we reviewed the fundamental principles of dynamics and introduced the concept of kinematics for rigid bodies. Now, we will delve deeper into the equations of motion for rigid bodies and explore their applications.



#### 19.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



While the kinematics of rigid bodies have many practical applications, they also present several challenges that must be addressed in order to accurately model and control these systems. One of the main challenges is dealing with the complexity of the equations of motion for rigid bodies. These equations involve multiple variables and parameters, making it difficult to analyze and predict the motion of a rigid body.



Another challenge is the accurate measurement and tracking of the motion of a rigid body. In order to control a rigid body, we must be able to accurately measure its position, velocity, and acceleration. However, due to factors such as sensor noise and external disturbances, it can be challenging to obtain precise measurements of these quantities.



Furthermore, the motion of a rigid body can be affected by external forces and torques, making it difficult to predict and control its behavior. These external forces and torques can come from a variety of sources, such as friction, air resistance, and contact with other objects. Accounting for these external influences is crucial in accurately modeling the dynamics of a rigid body.



In addition to these challenges, the kinematics of rigid bodies also have theoretical implications that must be considered. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is closely related to Poinsot's construction, can be difficult to calculate and analyze in certain cases. This can make it challenging to determine the stability of a rotating body.



## Further reading



For a more in-depth understanding of the challenges and solutions in modeling the kinematics of rigid bodies, we recommend the following resources:



- "Dynamics and Control of Rigid Bodies" by Roy Featherstone

- "Robot Dynamics and Control" by Mark W. Spong, Seth Hutchinson, and M. Vidyasagar

- "Introduction to Aerospace Engineering with a Flight Test Perspective" by Stephen Corda





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we explored the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and examine the equations that govern their motion. Understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies is crucial for accurately modeling and controlling their behavior.



#### 19.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



The dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion can be described by the equations of motion, which are derived from Newton's laws of motion. These equations involve multiple variables and parameters, making it challenging to analyze and predict the motion of a rigid body. However, by understanding the underlying principles and concepts, we can gain a deeper understanding of these equations and their applications.



One of the main challenges in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accurately measuring and tracking their motion. In order to control a rigid body, we must be able to measure its position, velocity, and acceleration. However, due to factors such as sensor noise and external disturbances, it can be difficult to obtain precise measurements of these quantities. This can lead to errors in the equations of motion and affect the accuracy of our predictions.



Another challenge in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accounting for external forces and torques. These external influences can come from a variety of sources, such as friction, air resistance, and contact with other objects. Neglecting these forces and torques can lead to inaccurate predictions of the motion of a rigid body.



In addition to these challenges, the kinematics of rigid bodies also have theoretical implications that must be considered. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is closely related to Poinsot's construction, can be difficult to calculate and analyze in certain cases. This can make it challenging to fully understand the behavior of a rigid body in motion.



Despite these challenges, the study of plane-motion dynamics of rigid bodies has many practical applications. It is used in fields such as robotics, aerospace engineering, and mechanical engineering to design and control complex systems. By understanding the underlying principles and challenges, we can effectively model and control the dynamics of rigid bodies in various applications.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we explored the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and examine the equations that govern their motion. Understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies is crucial for accurately modeling and controlling their behavior.



#### 19.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



The dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion can be described by the equations of motion, which are derived from Newton's laws of motion. These equations involve multiple variables and parameters, making it challenging to analyze and predict the motion of a rigid body. However, by understanding the underlying principles and concepts, we can gain a deeper understanding of these equations and their applications.



One of the main challenges in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accurately measuring and tracking their motion. In order to control a rigid body, we must be able to measure its position, velocity, and acceleration. However, due to factors such as sensor noise and external disturbances, it can be difficult to obtain precise measurements of these quantities. This can lead to errors in the equations of motion and affect the accuracy of our predictions.



Another challenge in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accounting for external forces and torques. These external influences can come from a variety of sources, such as friction, air resistance, and contact with other objects. Neglecting these forces and torques can lead to inaccurate predictions of the motion of a rigid body.



In addition to these challenges, the kinematics of rigid bodies also have theoretical implications that must be considered. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is closely related to Poinsot's construction, can be difficult to calculate and analyze in certain cases. This can make it challenging to fully understand and predict the motion of a rigid body.



### Subsection: 19.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



Despite these challenges, the study of plane-motion dynamics has many practical applications. One such application is in the field of spacecraft dynamics. By accurately modeling the dynamics of a spacecraft, we can better understand its behavior and make necessary adjustments to control its motion.



Another application of plane-motion dynamics is in the development of extended Kalman filters. These filters are used for state estimation in systems with both continuous-time models and discrete-time measurements. By incorporating the equations of motion and measurement models, we can improve the accuracy of our predictions and control of the system.



Furthermore, understanding plane-motion dynamics is crucial in the design and control of robotic systems. By accurately modeling the dynamics of a robot, we can optimize its performance and ensure its safety in various environments.



In summary, the study of plane-motion dynamics is essential for accurately modeling and controlling the behavior of rigid bodies. Despite its challenges, this field has many practical applications and continues to be an important area of research in the field of dynamics and control.





# Modeling Dynamics and Control II:



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



### Section: 19.3 Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we explored the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will delve into the dynamics of these systems and examine the equations that govern their motion. Understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies is crucial for accurately modeling and controlling their behavior.



#### 19.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



The dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion can be described by the equations of motion, which are derived from Newton's laws of motion. These equations involve multiple variables and parameters, making it challenging to analyze and predict the motion of a rigid body. However, by understanding the underlying principles and concepts, we can gain a deeper understanding of these equations and their applications.



One of the main challenges in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accurately measuring and tracking their motion. In order to control a rigid body, we must be able to measure its position, velocity, and acceleration. However, due to factors such as sensor noise and external disturbances, it can be difficult to obtain precise measurements of these quantities. This can lead to errors in the equations of motion and affect the accuracy of our predictions.



Another challenge in modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies is accounting for external forces and torques. These external influences can come from a variety of sources, such as friction, air resistance, and contact with other objects. Neglecting these forces and torques can lead to inaccurate predictions of the motion of a rigid body.



In addition to these challenges, the kinematics of rigid bodies also have theoretical implications that must be considered. For example, the concept of kinetic width, which is closely related to Poinsot's construction, can be difficult to calculate and analyze in certain cases. This can make it challenging to fully understand and predict the behavior of a rigid body in motion.



### Subsection: 19.3b Hierarchical Equations of Motion



In order to accurately model the dynamics of a rigid body, we must consider the hierarchical equations of motion (HEOM). These equations take into account the various forces and torques acting on a rigid body and how they affect its motion. The HEOM method is implemented in a number of freely available codes, making it a widely used tool in the study of rigid body dynamics.



One of the key advantages of the HEOM method is its flexibility. It allows for the incorporation of various external forces and torques, making it applicable to a wide range of systems. Additionally, the HEOM method can be used to study the dynamics of both simple and complex rigid bodies, making it a valuable tool for researchers and engineers.



### Subsection: 19.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



While the HEOM method is a powerful tool for modeling the dynamics of rigid bodies, there are still challenges that must be addressed. One of the main challenges is the computational complexity of the HEOM equations. As the number of variables and parameters increases, the equations become more complex and difficult to solve. This can make it challenging to analyze and predict the behavior of a rigid body in real-time.



Another challenge is the accuracy of the measurements used in the HEOM equations. As mentioned earlier, obtaining precise measurements of a rigid body's position, velocity, and acceleration can be difficult due to external disturbances and sensor noise. This can lead to errors in the equations and affect the accuracy of our predictions.



In addition, the HEOM method assumes that the rigid body is in continuous motion. However, in reality, there may be instances where the rigid body experiences sudden changes in motion, such as collisions or impacts. These discontinuities can make it challenging to accurately model the dynamics of the rigid body.



Despite these challenges, the HEOM method remains a valuable tool for studying the dynamics of rigid bodies. By understanding and addressing these challenges, we can continue to improve our understanding and control of these complex systems.

In this chapter, we have explored the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. We began by discussing the concept of inertia and how it relates to the motion of a rigid body. We then introduced the equations of motion for a rigid body, including the moment of inertia and the angular momentum. We also discussed the principle of conservation of angular momentum and how it can be applied to solve problems involving rigid bodies in motion.



Next, we delved into the dynamics of a rigid body in free fall, examining the forces and torques acting on the body and how they affect its motion. We also explored the concept of rotational kinetic energy and how it relates to the motion of a rigid body. Additionally, we discussed the concept of gyroscopic motion and its applications in various fields.



Finally, we concluded the chapter by discussing the dynamics of a rigid body in circular motion. We examined the forces and torques acting on the body and how they contribute to its motion. We also explored the concept of centripetal force and its relationship to circular motion. Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion and their applications in various scenarios.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction



In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. We learned about the various components of a dynamic system, such as mass, stiffness, and damping, and how they interact to produce motion. We also studied different types of control systems and their applications in regulating the behavior of a system.



In this chapter, we will build upon our understanding of dynamics and control by delving into the complexities of two-degree-of-freedom systems. These systems consist of two masses connected by springs and dampers, and they exhibit more complex behavior compared to single-degree-of-freedom systems. We will explore the vibrations of two-degree-of-freedom systems and how they can be modeled and controlled.



We will begin by discussing the basics of two-degree-of-freedom systems, including their equations of motion and natural frequencies. We will then move on to studying the different types of vibrations that can occur in these systems, such as free, forced, and damped vibrations. We will also explore the concept of resonance and its effects on two-degree-of-freedom systems.



Next, we will delve into the topic of control for two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will learn about the various control techniques that can be used to regulate the behavior of these systems, such as feedback control and feedforward control. We will also discuss the advantages and limitations of each control technique.



Finally, we will apply our knowledge of two-degree-of-freedom systems to real-world examples. We will study the vibrations of a car suspension system and how it can be controlled to improve ride comfort. We will also explore the vibrations of a building during an earthquake and how control systems can be used to mitigate the effects of these vibrations.



By the end of this chapter, you will have a thorough understanding of the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. You will be able to model and analyze the behavior of these systems and apply control techniques to regulate their motion. This knowledge will be valuable in various engineering fields, such as mechanical, aerospace, and civil engineering. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of two-degree-of-freedom systems!

### Section: 20.1 Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in 2.003, the introductory course on dynamics and control at MIT. This will serve as a refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.
